The Velvet Underground recorded their first album in a garage in St. Louis, Missouri. The album was produced by the band and released in 1967 by a small independent label, Verve Records. It was a critical and commercial success, and is generally considered to be the first album of the genre known as rock and roll. In addition to being influential, it is also considered to be one of the best albums ever made. The band later recorded several more albums, including White Light/White Heat, which was released in 1968, and Loaded, which was released in 1970. In the early 1970s, the band broke up, but reunited in the 1990s. Since then, the band has continued to record and perform.  The Velvet Underground were an American rock band formed in New York City in 1965. The band consisted of Lou Reed (lead vocals, guitar), Sterling Morrison (guitar), Maureen Tucker (bass, vocals), and Moe Tucker (drums, percussion). In the early 1960s, the band played a mixture of garage rock, art rock, and psychedelic rock. The band was short-lived, playing only nine concerts before breaking up in 1970. In 1972, however, the band reunited, releasing a self-titled album, The Velvet Underground, in 1973. Since then, the band has continued to record and perform. Aaron Turner is a post-metal musician from Massachusetts, USA, who is currently active as a solo artist. His debut album, The Great Misdirect, was released in 2001, followed by the critically acclaimed The Night Is Red in 2004, and the critically and commercially successful albums A Sun That Never Sets and The Cold Vein in 2008 and 2010, respectively. Aaron Turner is currently working on his sixth album with a release date set for 2013. In addition to his solo work, he is also a member of the band Lotus Eaters, which he formed in 2001 with drummer Dan Rincon and guitarist Tim Presley. They have released two studio albums, 2002's Let The End Begin and 2004's Sweet Oblivion, as well as a live album, A Night at the Theatre, in 2005. Their third album, Days of Abandon, was released in 2008.

Year of No Light, the fourth album by American post-metal band Year of No Light, was released on September 6, 2006, by Crucial Blast. It is a more melodic, cleaner sounding album than their previous releases. It was produced by the band and mixed by Andy Wallace. The album is a departure from the band's previous, more aggressive sound. The album was recorded and mixed by the band in a small studio in New York City. It was mastered by Joe Gastwirt. The album was the band's first release on the label. The album's title was a reference to the Nordic mythology of the Norse goddess of winter, Hel.

Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, located in the city of Madrid, and is the principal hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia. It is owned by the Spanish air navigation service provider ENAIRE, a public company owned by the Ministry of Development. The airport is located in the municipalities of Madrid and Alcobendas, and serves the city of Madrid. It is the sixth-busiest airport in Europe by passenger traffic and the eighth-busiest airport in Europe by aircraft movements. In 2015, Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport handled a total of 41.2 million passengers, making it the third-busiest airport in Europe by passenger traffic. It also handled 5.7 million tonnes of freight, making it the fifth-busiest airport in Europe by freight traffic. In 2010, the airport was the busiest airport in the world by aircraft movements. The airport is the busiest in Spain and the second-busiest in Europe by passenger traffic. In 2017, the airport handled 45.2 million passengers and handled 6.3 million tonnes of freight, making it the sixth-busiest airport in Europe by freight traffic. It is the main hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia. The airport is the sixth-busiest airport in Europe by passenger traffic and the eighth-busiest airport in Europe by aircraft movements. The Stavropolis asteroid was discovered on October 1, 2014 by the Catalina Sky Survey. It has a diameter of about 9.5 kilometers and an estimated mass of about 3.9 million tons. Stavropolis is a member of the Koronis family and orbits the Sun in the outer main-belt at a distance of 2.8–3.6 AU once every 4 years and 6 months (1,637 days; semi-major axis of 3.04 AU). Its orbit has an eccentricity of 0.14 and an inclination of 14° with respect to the ecliptic. The body's observation arc begins with its official discovery observation at Palomar.  The asteroid was named for the Stavropol Region in Russia. The official naming citation was mentioned in The Names of the Minor Planets by Paul Herget in 1955 (H 127).  Stavropolis was initially designated as 2014 WY93, and later as 2015 FC, before being renamed to its present name.  In July 2016, Stavropolis reached its perihelion (closest approach to the Sun) at a distance of 0.25 AU, which is about 42.5 million kilometers. This occurred on 31 July 2016 (JD 2457600.5), when the asteroid was at a brightness of magnitude 17.8.  On 20 September 2016, Stavropolis will pass within 0.0024 AU (about 8.7 million kilometers) of Mars. The two objects will have their closest approach at 12:28 UTC. Stavropolis will be 0.09 magnitude fainter than Mars, and about 0.02 magnitude fainter than the brightest stars in the night sky. Stavropolis will be the brightest minor planet to pass so close to Mars since the flyby of asteroid (1685) Lépida on 28 July 2005.  A small lunar occultation of Stavropolis will occur on 25 October 2016, when the asteroid will pass between the Earth and the Moon. The occultation will begin at approximately 23:22 UTC, and will last for about 3.2 minutes.  Stavropolis will be at its brightest in early March 2017, when it will reach an apparent magnitude of about 14.7. It will also be at its closest to Earth on that date, at a distance of 0.084 AU (about 12.2 million kilometers).  The Stavropolis asteroid will pass 0.01 AU (about 14.6 million kilometers

Olga Nikolaevna Bondareva (born April 27, 1937 in Saint Petersburg, Russia) is a Russian mathematician known for her work on the foundations of algebraic geometry, including the Bondarev–Shapley theorem. She was awarded the Fields Medal in 1970. She was the first female to be awarded the Fields Medal.
The Fellowship of the Ring is a 1954 fantasy novel by English author J. R. R. Tolkien. It is the first volume in The Lord of the Rings trilogy, and was followed by The Two Towers and The Return of the King. The novel is set in the fictional world of Middle-earth, Tolkien's imagined past. It is written in a subgenre of fantasy known as high fantasy. It is also set in a secondary world that is in many ways a historical reconstruction of medieval Europe, with characters such as King Arthur, the Knights of the Round Table, and the Holy Grail. The story concerns the journey of a group of friends called the Fellowship of the Ring, led by the hobbit Frodo Baggins, who must destroy a powerful ring made by the Dark Lord Sauron. This is the only way to prevent the re-emergence of Sauron and the return of the Dark Lord. The Fellowship of the Ring was published in the United Kingdom on 21 September 1954, and in the United States on 22 November 1954. It was preceded by The Hobbit, which was published in 1937. The book was the first of Tolkien's writings to be published in the United States, after a friend of his had sent a copy to the editor of Harper & Brothers, who agreed to publish it. It is considered to be one of the most important works of fantasy of the twentieth century. It has been translated into over 50 languages. Tolkien intended to publish the book under his own name, but Harper & Brothers insisted that he use a pseudonym, so he chose J. R. R. Tolkien, which stood for his initials, John Ronald Reuel Tolkien. Tolkien later explained that he chose the pseudonym because he had been called "Roy" by his friends and family, and he wanted to be addressed as "Reuel". The Fellowship of the Ring was a commercial success. It won the Lewis Carroll Shelf Award in 1955, and was nominated for the Carnegie Medal the same year. In 2001, it was listed by the Modern Library as one of the 100 best English-language novels of the 20th century. In 2003, it was listed as the third best-selling book of the 20th century in the United States, with over 10 million copies sold. The novel was adapted into a film trilogy in the 1980s and 1990s. AmeriGas, the nation's leader in propane, provides fuel, fueling, and convenience products and services to residential, commercial, industrial, agricultural, and automotive customers. The company has more than 3,600 retail locations throughout the United States and its territories, and is the largest wholesale distributor of propane in the United States, selling an average of more than 9 billion gallons of propane annually. Headquartered in King of Prussia, Pennsylvania, AmeriGas is a subsidiary of UGI Corporation, a Fortune 500 company with annual revenues of approximately $8.5 billion. The company is committed to protecting the environment through the use of propane as a clean-burning, domestically produced fuel.
The Stavropolis, discovered by Grigory Neujmin in 1929, is a planet in the Solar System. It is in orbit around the Sun. The Stavropolis has a radius of 0.45 times the radius of the Earth, a surface gravity of 0.52 times the gravity of the Earth, and a mass of 0.2 times the mass of the Earth. The Stavropolis is named after the city of Stavropol, in the Russian Federation.  The Stavropolis has an aphelion distance of 418476000000.0 km from the Sun, an orbital period of 1249.6 days, an inclination of 1.7 degrees, an eccentricity of 0.49, and a semi-major axis of 4.1 AU. Its mass is 0.2 times the mass of the Earth.  The Stavropolis is classified as a Dwarf planet in the Solar System.

Bananaman, the much-loved animated comedy, is returning to television for the first time in 25 years. The show will be broadcast on BBC Two next year. The show, starring Bill Oddie as the lead character, was created by Steve Bright in the late 1970s. The original run of the show, which was produced by the BBC, ran from 1986 to 1992. It was created by Steve Bright, who also played the lead character, and Bill Oddie, who voiced the title character. The ALCO RS-3 was built by Montreal Locomotive Works (MLW) in Montreal, Canada from 1950 until 1956. It is a two-piece body, stainless steel, articulated locomotive with a four-wheel leading truck and a six-wheel trailing truck. The leading truck is connected to the trailing truck by a coupler, which can be moved inboard to reduce the length of the locomotive to less than 120 feet (37 m) for passing through tunnels and under bridges. The leading truck has a brake cylinder and a horn; the trailing truck has a brake cylinder and a bell. The locomotive has a power output of 3,600 hp (2,600 kW) and a top speed of 80 mph (130 km/h). The ALCO RS-3 was built to operate on the Canadian National Railway (CNR) and Canadian Pacific Railway (CPR) and is similar to the ALCO RS-2, but with a heavier frame and larger diameter wheels.

Expect a Miracle is an album by the American singer-songwriter Carole King. It was released on February 8, 1971, by Columbia Records as her second solo album, following Tapestry (1971). The album was produced by Phil Spector and was recorded in the autumn of 1970. King co-wrote all the songs on the album, with the exception of "I've Got a Feeling About You", which was written by King and Gerry Goffin. The album's lead single, "(You Make Me Feel Like) A Natural Woman", became one of King's most successful songs, and is widely considered one of her signature songs.

Olga Nikolaevna Bondareva (born 30 August 1934 in Leningrad, Russia) is a Soviet mathematician. She is known for her contributions to algebraic geometry, including the proof of the Mordell–Weil theorem for elliptic curves, and for the proof of the existence of global holomorphic solutions of the Korteweg–de Vries equation. She is the author of several books and monographs on algebraic geometry. She is a member of the Russian Academy of Sciences. In 2012, she became a member of the National Academy of Sciences of Ukraine. Bondareva was born in Leningrad. She graduated from Leningrad State University in 1958 with a degree in mathematics. She continued her education in Moscow, at the Steklov Institute of Mathematics, from which she received a Ph.D. degree in 1965. She was awarded a Doctor of Sciences degree in 1973. She was a professor at the Steklov Institute from 1965 to 1986, when she became a professor at the Department of Mathematics of the Leningrad State University. She was elected as a corresponding member of the Russian Academy of Sciences in 1983, and as a full member in 1990. Bondareva is best known for the proof of the Mordell–Weil theorem for elliptic curves (with Vladimir Popov), and for the proof of the existence of global holomorphic solutions of the Korteweg–de Vries equation (with Igor Shafarevich). She also made important contributions to algebraic geometry and the theory of algebraic curves. In addition, she worked on the theory of algebraic functions of one variable and on the theory of automorphic functions. In her book on the Mordell–Weil theorem, she introduced the notion of a canonical subgroup of an abelian variety. In her book on algebraic curves, she defined a canonical divisor on an algebraic curve, which she called the "canonical class". She also introduced the notions of a "canonical" map and of a "canonical" covering. She proved that a canonical map is birational, and that it is defined by a canonical covering. In a paper from 1968, she proved that any plane algebraic curve of degree at least 3 has infinitely many points of inflection. She also introduced the notion of an inflectional cover in her work on the Mordell–Weil theorem. She proved that any elliptic curve can be realized as an inflectional cover of a curve of genus 1. Sludge metal is a subgenre of heavy metal music that originated in the United States in the late 1980s. Southern sludge metal is a subgenre of sludge metal which originated in the southern United States.  It is often considered to be a slower, dirtier, and more extreme form of doom metal. It is also considered to be a variant of stoner metal.  The term was coined by Greg Prato in a 1988 article for the fanzine Abrasive Wheels, describing the music of Black Flag, Eyehategod, and the Melvins. Prato's term was used in the 1991 book The Decibel Magazine Guide to Heavy Metal by Sean Pecknold, which popularized the term.  The term was later applied to bands like Crowbar, Down, Eyehategod, Mastodon, High on Fire, and Baroness. It is also used to describe the music of bands like Corrosion of Conformity, Crowbar, Down, Eyehategod, Mastodon, High on Fire, and Baroness. Death on a Factory Farm was a television documentary miniseries, directed by Sarah Teale and Tom Simon, and broadcasted by HBO in 2011. The series was written by Geof Bartz and executive produced by Ken Burns and Lynn Novick. The miniseries is about the rise of industrial agriculture and its effect on the environment, food, and health, and features interviews with former US Secretary of Agriculture Henry A. Wallace, environmental activist Bill McKibben, food scientist Michael Pollan, and many other experts in the fields of farming and health. The miniseries was nominated for the Primetime Emmy Award for Outstanding Informational Series in 2012.

The Quine Tapes is the debut studio album by American singer-songwriter J. D. Considine. It was released in 1990 on the independent label Bootleg Series Volume 1, founded by Considine and his brother, Bob Considine, in 1988. The album was produced by Mark Nevers, who had worked with the band on their previous album, Under the Influence, and was recorded at various locations, including a church and Considine's house. The album was well received by critics and consistently appeared on critics' "best of" lists, with AllMusic calling it "a landmark in the history of indie-rock".

The Honeymoon Killers are an American band from Phoenix, Arizona. They formed in 1984 and have released five studio albums, two compilation albums and two EPs. Their sound is considered to be part of the alternative rock genre. They have toured with several well-known artists, including R.E.M., Red Hot Chili Peppers, The Pixies, The Jesus and Mary Chain, Nirvana, and The Smashing Pumpkins. The Honeymoon Killers’ debut album, Let It Breed, was released in 1985. The album was a commercial success, reaching number one on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number six on the UK Albums Chart. The album was also nominated for the Mercury Music Prize in 1986. The Honeymoon Killers’ second album, The Smell of Us, was released in 1988. It was a commercial success, reaching number two on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number three on the UK Albums Chart. The album was also nominated for the Mercury Music Prize in 1989. The Honeymoon Killers’ third album, Turn Me On, was released in 1991. It was a commercial success, reaching number one on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number six on the UK Albums Chart. The album was also nominated for the Mercury Music Prize in 1992. The Honeymoon Killers’ fourth album, Beautiful, was released in 1995. It was a commercial success, reaching number two on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number three on the UK Albums Chart. The album was also nominated for the Mercury Music Prize in 1996. The Honeymoon Killers’ fifth album, A Different Kind of Tension, was released in 2001. It was a commercial success, reaching number one on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number five on the UK Albums Chart. The album was also nominated for the Mercury Music Prize in 2002. In 2003, the band released a compilation album, The Best of the Honeymoon Killers. The album reached number seven on the UK Independent Albums Chart and number eight on the UK Albums Chart. In 2006, The Honeymoon Killers were inducted into the Independent Music Companies Association Hall of Fame. Ciudad Ayala is a city in the Philippines. It is the capital of the province of Cagayan and the center of the Cagayan Valley Region. It is located in the northeastern part of the island of Luzon, about 40 kilometers south of the capital, Manila. The city is also the seat of the Cagayan Valley Regional Trial Court, which is the trial court of the Cagayan Valley Region. It is also the site of the Cagayan Valley College, a private college in the Philippines, and the Ayala Museum. Ciudad Ayala is the third most populous city in the Philippines. The city has a population of 162,250 as of the 2015 Census. Ciudad Ayala is located on the banks of the Cagayan River, about 60 kilometers south of the mouth of the river. The Cagayan River is one of the major rivers in the Philippines, running from the slopes of the Sierra Madre mountain range in northern Luzon to the South China Sea. The city is administered by the City Mayor, who is elected every six years by the citizens of the city. The city government is a council-manager government, where the mayor appoints the city council and the council appoints the city manager. The city council is composed of the mayor, six city councilors, and the city treasurer. The city government is located at the Municipal Building, which is located in the city center. Ciudad Ayala is the capital of the province of Cagayan. The province is located in the northeastern part of the island of Luzon, in the Philippines. The province has a total area of 1,835.3 square kilometers. It is bounded on the north by the province of Ilocos Norte, on the south by the provinces of Ilocos Sur and La Union, on the east by the province of Pangasinan, and on the west by the provinces of Nueva Ecija and Batangas. The province of Cagayan is divided into 17 barangays, which are administered by the barangay captain, who is elected every three years by the citizens of the barangay. The province is administered by the Provincial Board, which is composed of the governor, the provincial vice governor, the secretary, and the treasurer. The Stavropolis, also known as 47 Ursae Majoris, is a bright star in Ursa Major. It is located at 11.3 light-years from Earth. It has a magnitude of 11.5 and is visible to the naked eye under good conditions. Its proper name is Tethys, but it is also known as Stavropolis, which means 'beautiful city' in Greek, or the 'City of the Gods'. Its spectrum is classified as F0. It is a blue-white main sequence star, with a temperature of about 9,000 K. It is the 10th brightest star in the night sky and is one of the brightest stars in the Ursa Major constellation. The Stavropolis is also known as the 'Star of God'. It is a binary star system, with an orbital period of 1249.6 days. The Stavropolis is a spectroscopic binary star, and is a member of the Ursa Major moving group. It has an apsidal motion of 0.0 seconds of arc per year and an eccentricity of 0.0001. It has an absolute magnitude of 11.5.

The Velvet Underground was an American rock band formed in 1964 in New York City. It was one of the most influential rock bands of the 1960s and 1970s.[1] The band was active for three years, releasing two albums, before splitting up in 1967. In the early 1990s the band was inducted into the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame, and the band's albums were re-released and re-evaluated in the light of their importance to rock music. The band's original line-up was Lou Reed on lead vocals and guitar, John Cale on keyboards and viola, Sterling Morrison on bass guitar, and Maureen Tucker on drums and percussion. Reed and Cale had met in New York City in the early 1960s, and they began performing together in 1963. Reed had been a member of the New York Dolls, and Cale had been a member of The Modern Lovers. Morrison and Tucker were recruited by Reed and Cale in 1964. The band's first album, The Velvet Underground & Nico, was released in March 1967. The album was commercially unsuccessful, but it has since been widely regarded as a classic and has sold millions of copies. The band's second album, White Light/White Heat, was released in August 1967. The band's third album, The Velvet Underground, was released in February 1969, but the band was in disarray by then, with Reed and Morrison leaving the band and being replaced by Doug Yule and Angus Maclise respectively. Reed's first solo album, Transformer, was released in June 1972. The Velvet Underground played their last show in New York City on February 9, 1970, and then disbanded. Reed and Cale later reformed the band in the late 1970s, releasing a series of albums, and continuing to perform. The band was inducted into the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame in 1991. The band is now considered a classic rock band.[2] The band's music, often classified as proto-punk, was influenced by the writings of William S. Burroughs, and by the music of artists such as Charles Mingus, Duke Ellington, and Thelonious Monk.[3][4] The band's music was also influenced by the sounds of the New York City underground music scene, which was dominated by the blues, R&B, and jazz. The Stavropolis asteroid was discovered on October 1, 2003 by Russian astronomers at the Zelenchukskaya station of the Crimean Astrophysical Observatory in Ukraine. It has a diameter of about 45.0 km and is a member of the Apollo asteroid group. Its orbit has a semi-major axis of 2.6 AU, an eccentricity of 0.18, and an inclination of 7.9 degrees. It is named after the city of Stavropol, located in the North Caucasus region of Russia. Its next close approach to the Earth is on August 12, 2039. It is numbered 1147. Stavropolis is classified as a main-belt asteroid. It is located in the inner part of the main-belt, at a distance of 2.59 AU from the Sun, and at a distance of 1.12 AU from the Earth. Stavropolis is a retrograde and eccentric asteroid. Its orbital period is 1249.6 days. Its orbit has an eccentricity of 0.18 and an inclination of 7.9 degrees with respect to the ecliptic. This volume, Bootleg Series Volume 1, contains three performances, recorded at the Chicago Auditorium in Chicago, Illinois, in 1973. The recordings were made by Richard Lloyd. The performances include "See You," a song that later appeared on the Richard Hell and the Voidoids album Blank Generation (1976), and "Child of the Revolution," a song that later appeared on the Television Personalities album Toys in the Attic (1975). The performances were originally released on the Quine Tapes bootleg series, which consists of multiple volumes. The recordings were officially released on the compilation album The Quine Tapes (2000).

Agremiação Sportiva Arapiraquense is a football club in Arapiraca, Alagoas. It plays in Campeonato Brasileiro Série C, the third tier of the Brazilian football league system. It was founded on November 3, 2005, and its home stadium is the Estádio Coaracy da Mata Fonseca, which has a capacity of 17,000 people. In the 2014 season, it was promoted to Série C for the first time.  In 2015, Agremiação Sportiva Arapiraquense competed in Campeonato Brasileiro Série C for the first time. In the fourth round, it played against ASA, and won 1–0. In the fifth round, it played against ABC, and won 1–0. In the sixth round, it played against Santa Cruz, and lost 3–2. In the seventh round, it played against Remo, and lost 3–2.  Agremiação Sportiva Arapiraquense finished the 2015 season in the 12th position. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Madrid, Spain. It is located in the municipality of Alcobendas, in the Community of Madrid, about 12 kilometres (7.5 mi) north of the centre of Madrid. It is the second busiest airport in Spain, after Barcelona-El Prat Airport, and the ninth busiest in Europe in terms of passenger traffic. The airport is owned and operated by Aena, the Spanish state-owned company that owns and operates all Spanish airports. The airport has two runways, 14R/32L (3,500 m × 60 m) and 14L/32R (3,500 m × 60 m). The airport's elevation is 610 metres (2,000 ft) above sea level. It is the most populous municipality in the metropolitan area of Madrid, with a population of approximately 141,000 in 2017. The municipality of Alcobendas has an area of 12.7 km² (4.9 sq mi). Expect a Miracle is an instrumental album by American composer John Williams. It was released in the United States on June 8, 2012, by Sony Classical. It is the soundtrack to the 2012 film The Adventures of Tintin: The Secret of the Unicorn. The album was written by Williams and produced by his son, John Williams, Jr. It was recorded in London in 2011, and was Williams's first instrumental album in nearly 30 years. The album received a Grammy Award nomination for Best Instrumental Composition. In 2012, it was named the best album of the year by the International Classical Music Awards and was a finalist for the 2012 Classic FM Gramophone Award. The album's title track, "Expect a Miracle", is an orchestral piece that was composed for the film and performed by the London Symphony Orchestra, conducted by Williams. It was released as a single on iTunes and Spotify. The album also includes a piece called "The Hunt for Red October", a theme from the film Red October, and a piece called "Russian Lullaby", which was composed for the film The Hunt for Red October and later used as the theme for the television series The Americans. The album also contains a track called "Prologue", which features a string arrangement of Williams's theme from the film Jaws. The album's closing track, "The Adventures of Tintin", is a musical narration of the film's story, featuring a narration by Williams and a chorus of the London Symphony Orchestra. "Expect a Miracle" was released on vinyl and CD, and was made available for digital download on June 8, 2012. The album has been released in the United Kingdom, France, and Japan.
Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, located in the city of Madrid, and is the principal hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia. It is located approximately 15 kilometres (9.3 mi) north of downtown Madrid, and is the second-busiest airport in Spain, after Barcelona-El Prat Airport, serving a total of over 40 million passengers per year. It is owned by the Spanish state-owned company Aena. The airport is named after Adolfo Suárez González, the first president of the democratic government of Spain after the death of Francisco Franco. Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main hub for Iberia, Air Europa, Vueling and Air Nostrum. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is located in the municipality of Alcobendas, which is part of the Community of Madrid. The airport is located within the metropolitan area of Madrid, and is accessible via the M-12 motorway, and by the Metro and Cercanías Madrid commuter rail services. The airport is served by two stations on Line 8 of the Metro, and by the Cercanías Madrid commuter rail service at the Barajas station. The airport is situated on the banks of the Rio Jarama, which flows into the Manzanares River, near the Jarama reservoir. The airport is located on a former marshy plain, with the airport building built on reclaimed land. The elevation above the sea level is 610 metres (2,000 ft). The airport is protected by the Barajas Control Area, which is under the jurisdiction of the Spanish Air Force. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport was officially opened on 12 December 1969. The airport is currently owned and operated by Aena. It is the principal airport of the Spanish Air Force, and is also used for military transport operations. The airport is also a base for Spanish military and civil aircraft. The airport is currently the second-busiest airport in Spain, after Barcelona-El Prat Airport, serving a total of over 40 million passengers per year. Mermaid Train Song The Mermaid Train Song is a Pop rock song by the American rock band Train. It was released as the third and final single from their third studio album, Dynamite (2001). The song was written by lead singer Pat Monahan, with the lyrics inspired by his time working at a Chicago restaurant named The Mermaid, and features a sample of The Drifters' 1962 song, "Save the Last Dance for Me". The song was released in the United States on September 25, 2001, by Columbia Records as a CD single, with the B-side track "Southern Air". It was a hit on the country charts, peaking at number one on the Billboard Hot Country Singles & Tracks chart and number two on the Country Airplay chart. The song also peaked at number one on the Canadian Hot 100 chart and at number three on the US Billboard Hot 100 chart, making it the band's first top-five single. The song has since been certified Gold by the RIAA for sales of over 500,000 copies in the United States. The song was also a hit in the United Kingdom, peaking at number six on the UK Singles Chart and at number five on the UK Country Chart. The song was certified Silver by the BPI for sales of over 200,000 copies in the United Kingdom. The song was performed live for the first time on The Oprah Winfrey Show on November 7, 2001, and was included on the band's 2002 concert DVD Live from Earth. The song was also included on the soundtrack for the 2002 film The Italian Job. The song was also included on the band's 2003 compilation album, Greatest Hits: 18 Kids. It was also included on the band's 2003 live album, The Best of Train: Live at the Greek. It was also included on the band's 2011 compilation album, Greatest Hits: 18 Kids. Mermaid Train Song" was released as a single in Australia and New Zealand in 2003, and was a hit in both countries, reaching number one on the Australian Singles Chart and number four on the New Zealand Singles Chart. Darinka Dentcheva is an optimization researcher with a background in mathematics. Her professional interests include stochastic programming, combinatorial optimization and mathematical modelling of real-world problems. She has worked on a number of projects, both theoretical and practical, with industry and the European Commission. She has taught optimization at Humboldt University, Berlin, and at the University of Bucharest, Romania.  Darinka Dentcheva was a student of Andrzej Ruszczyński, a professor in the Department of Mathematics at Humboldt University. She was influenced by Ruszczyński’s work on the theory of polyhedra and the development of the software package CPLEX for solving linear programming problems. She is currently working on a new version of CPLEX.

Death on a Factory Farm was a television documentary miniseries, directed by Tom Simon, that aired on HBO in 2002. It is based on the book of the same name by Annie MacDonald, who also served as executive producer of the television miniseries. The miniseries was executive produced by Tom Simon and Geof Bartz, and produced by Sarah Teale. The miniseries was written by Tom Simon, Geof Bartz, and Jamie Lawrence, and was scored by Jamie Lawrence. The miniseries was filmed in Vancouver, British Columbia, Canada. The miniseries was broadcast on HBO on September 27, 2002, and consisted of four episodes. The miniseries received a total of five Primetime Emmy Award nominations, including Outstanding Documentary or Nonfiction Series and Outstanding Writing for a Miniseries, Movie or a Dramatic Special for Tom Simon and Geof Bartz, and Outstanding Music Composition for a Miniseries or a Special for Jamie Lawrence. The miniseries was followed by a documentary series, which was broadcast on HBO from May 13, 2003 to June 8, 2003, consisting of four episodes. The miniseries is about the history of factory farming and its effects on the environment, the animals, and the people who work on factory farms. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is operated by ENAIRE, the Spanish air navigation service provider. The airport is located in Alcobendas, a town in the Community of Madrid, Spain. It is the main airport of Spain and the second largest airport in the European Union by passenger traffic. The airport is named after the former Spanish President Adolfo Suárez. It is the main hub of Iberia and Iberia Express, the flag carrier and its low-cost subsidiary respectively. It is also the main hub for Air Europa, a Spanish low-cost airline. The airport is also the main hub of the Spanish low-cost carrier Vueling, as well as the main hub of the Spanish regional airline Air Nostrum. The airport is the seventh busiest airport in Europe and the 24th busiest airport in the world by passenger traffic. The airport has three runways, with a combined length of 4,349 metres (14,150 ft). It is one of the busiest airports in the world by take-offs and landings, with over 600,000 take-offs and landings a year. Madrid is the capital of Spain. It is the third largest city in the European Union by population, and the largest city in Spain. It is located in the Community of Madrid, in the centre of the Iberian Peninsula, in the heart of the historical region of Castile. Madrid is the political, administrative, economic, and cultural centre of Spain. It is also the fifth largest city in the European Union by population within the administrative borders of the city. The city of Madrid is home to the headquarters of the Spanish Government and most of the Spanish ministries. Madrid is the seat of the Spanish National Court, the Spanish Royal Academy of History, the National Library of Spain, and the headquarters of the Spanish Stock Exchange. Madrid is also the capital of the autonomous community of Madrid, the most populated of Spain's 17 autonomous communities. It is also the most populated province of Spain, with a population of 6,489,449, according to the 2012 census. Spain is a country in Southwestern Europe, bordered by the Atlantic Ocean to the west, France to the north, the Mediterranean Sea to the east, and Portugal to the south. It is a constitutional monarchy, with a parliamentary system of government. It is a unitary state with autonomous regions. It is a developed country with a highly developed economy. The Stavropolis, discovered on 11 June 1929, is a minor planet orbiting the Sun in the outer main-belt at a distance of 2.8 AU. It has an absolute magnitude of 11.5. The body's observation arc begins with its official discovery observation in 1929, and no precovery has been taken since.  As no precovery has been taken, the following properties are based on the discovery observations. The Stavropolis measures 12.9 kilometers in diameter, and its surface has been characterized as both heavily cratered and relatively smooth. It has a rotation period of 1249.6 hours. It is an S-type asteroid.  The Stavropolis is named after Stavropol, a city in the southern Russian Federation. The official naming citation was mentioned in The Names of the Minor Planets by Paul Herget in 1955 (H 110).  The Stavropolis has an orbital period of 12 years and 7 months (1249.6 days), and an eccentricity of 0.17. It has an absolute magnitude of 11.5, and is therefore visible with the naked eye under ideal conditions. Expect a Miracle is an instrumental album by American composer John Williams. It was released in the United States on June 8, 2012, by Sony Classical. It is the soundtrack to the 2012 film The Adventures of Tintin: The Secret of the Unicorn. The album was written by Williams and produced by his son, John Williams, Jr. It was recorded in London in 2011, and was Williams's first instrumental album in 30 years. Williams said of the album, "I wanted to make a film music album that would be a real contrast to the 'Star Wars' soundtracks. I wanted to write something that was very different in style and feeling from anything I've done before."  The album's first single, "Miracle of Flight", was released on April 10, 2012. It reached number ten on the Billboard classical chart. The album was released in the United Kingdom on June 16, 2012.  On June 7, 2012, Williams was awarded the Polar Music Prize, which he accepted in the form of a €100,000 donation to the International Music Council. He was awarded the prize for his "immense contribution to the development of film music". In August 2012, Williams was made an honorary citizen of the town of Saint-Nazaire in France, where the action of The Adventures of Tintin takes place. He was also made an honorary citizen of the town of Brussels, Belgium, the birthplace of Hergé, the creator of Tintin.  The album debuted at number one on the Billboard classical album chart, selling more than 26,000 copies in its first week. It was the best-selling classical album of 2012 in the United States.  The album received generally favorable reviews. Reviewer David Hurwitz wrote, "Expect a Miracle is a stunning, highly personal statement of a gifted film composer and master orchestrator at the height of his powers."

The ALCO RS-3 is a class of locomotive built by the Montreal Locomotive Works. The RS-3 was built in two series, the first of which was built between 1948 and 1953 and the second between 1953 and 1957. The first series had a total of 1,115 locomotives built. The second series was built in two batches, the first of which was built between 1953 and 1955 and the second between 1955 and 1957. The second series had a total of 769 locomotives built. The first series was used on the Canadian National Railways (CNR) and the second series was used on the Canadian Pacific Railway (CPR). The RS-3s are among the most common locomotives in Canada. They were also exported to the United States, where they are known as the GP7. The RS-3 is the first diesel locomotive to be used in Canada. It is also the first diesel locomotive to be used on the CPR. The RS-3 was also used in the United States, where it was used on the Burlington Northern Railroad and the Missouri Pacific Railroad. The RS-3s were retired from service in Canada in the 1980s, with the last locomotives being retired in 1985. They were replaced by the ALCO RS-2, which is a class of locomotive that is similar to the RS-3.

It is the largest academic building on the campus. The building was constructed by the College of William and Mary to house the College's School of Business, which moved from its former location in the College's old Main Building in downtown Williamsburg. Brandon Carter, a professor of physics at the University of Texas at Austin, is a leading expert in general relativity, the theory of space, time, and gravity. He has published more than 200 papers on the subject and is a pioneer in developing new techniques for calculating the orbits of stars and planets around black holes. He also pioneered the use of computers in solving equations of general relativity and helped to develop the theory of gravitational waves. Carter's most famous work is the "Carter constant," a term used to describe the mathematical relationship between the energy of a star and the other variables that describe the star's orbit around a black hole. Carter's work on general relativity earned him a Pulitzer Prize for science reporting in 1985. Carter was also a reporter for The New York Times and served as a science editor for the newspaper. He was awarded the John Simon Guggenheim Memorial Fellowship in 1981 and the American Association for the Advancement of Science's Crafoord Prize in 1989. Carter was elected to the National Academy of Sciences in 1990 and to the American Academy of Arts and Sciences in 1993. Carter's most recent book is "Black Holes, Wormholes, and Time Warps: Einstein's Outrageous Legacy." Carter was born in 1942 in Kansas City, Missouri, and received his doctorate in physics from the University of California, Berkeley. He has also taught at the University of Texas at Austin and at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology. He is married to the author of the children's book series "The Magic School Bus."

The ALCO RS-3 was an American-built lightweight, four-door, four-passenger luxury car. It was based on the Fargo-Mohawk car, which was itself based on the Studebaker Lark. The RS-3 was produced in the United States by the Alcoa Automotive Company from 1950 to 1956. It was the first car in the world to use a monocoque body made of aluminum. The ALCO RS-3 was also the first car to use a vacuum-controlled thermostat, the first car to use a fuel injection system, and the first car to use a hydraulic braking system. The RS-3 was also one of the first cars to use a front-wheel drive layout. The RS-3 was named for its 3.0 L (2,953 cc) engine, which was derived from the Fargo-Mohawk's 2.9 L (2,875 cc) engine. The RS-3 was offered in two versions: the base model with a 145.7-inch (3,600 mm) wheelbase, and a longer model with a 153.3-inch (4,000 mm) wheelbase. The base model was offered in two trim levels: the Deluxe and the Custom. The Custom was a more sporty model, with a heavier suspension and a larger engine. The base model was offered with a 165.7-inch (4,630 mm) or a 169.6-inch (4,800 mm) wheelbase. The 169.6-inch wheelbase was also available with a V8 engine. The longer model was offered in the Custom trim level. The longer model was also offered with a V8 engine. The V8 engine was a 265.1-inch (5,000 mm) wheelbase model. The RS-3 was offered in two colors: white or green. The RS-3 was produced in several different states, including New York, Ohio, Michigan, and Pennsylvania. The RS-3 was also produced in Canada, and in England. The RS-3 was produced from 1950 to 1956. The RS-3 was replaced by the ALCO RS-4, which was also based on the Studebaker Lark. The Velvet Underground & Nico is a 1966 album by the American rock band the Velvet Underground, originally released on the Verve label. The album was produced by former band member Lou Reed and engineered by Andy Warhol. It was the only album released during the band's existence. The album was originally intended to be a single, but the band's record company insisted on releasing it as a double album, which was later seen as a classic rock album. The album is regarded as one of the most influential albums of all time, and is often cited as the first concept album. The album is also notable for being one of the first rock albums to include extensive use of avant-garde music. It is considered to be one of the first albums to feature artwork as integral to the album's concept as the music itself. The album was released in the UK as a double album, with the second disc containing two tracks by Nico, who also appeared in Warhol's film of the same name. The Velvet Underground & Nico was certified Gold by the RIAA on December 14, 2003, and has since been certified Platinum. In 2015, Rolling Stone magazine ranked the album at number 48 on its list of the 500 Greatest Albums of All Time. Ciudad Ayala is a city in the Philippines. It is the capital of the province of Cagayan and the center of the Cagayan Valley Region. It is located in the northeastern part of the island of Luzon, about 40 kilometers south of the capital, Manila. The city is also the seat of the Cagayan Valley Regional Trial Court, which is the trial court of the Cagayan Valley Region. It is also the site of the Regional Trial Court of the Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao. The city is also the home of the University of the Philippines Cayetano Heredia in Cagayan de Oro City, the second-largest campus of the University of the Philippines System.  The city was founded in 1902 as a stopover point along the railroad connecting Manila and the cities of Cagayan and Isabela. The first settler in the area was Vicente S. Sandoval. The city was named after Don Juan de Ayala, the Governor-General of the Philippine Islands at the time. It was formerly a barrio of Cagayan, a town located about ten kilometers to the north of the city. In October 1945, it became a separate municipality from Cagayan. The city was elevated to a chartered city status in 1996.

Alan Shepard was born on November 18, 1923, in West Lebanon, New Hampshire. His father, John Shepard, was a school principal, and his mother, Katharine E. Shepard, was a schoolteacher. Shepard was the second of three children, and the only son. He grew up on a farm near Lebanon. Shepard attended Lebanon High School, where he was active in sports, especially baseball, and was a member of the student council. He graduated in 1941, and was awarded a football scholarship to the University of New Hampshire. After his freshman year, he joined the U.S. Navy in 1942, and served as a pilot in the South Pacific during World War II. After the war, he returned to the University of New Hampshire, and graduated with a bachelor's degree in 1947. He enrolled at the Naval War College in Newport, Rhode Island, where he graduated with a master's degree in international relations in 1949. After graduating, Shepard was commissioned as an ensign in the United States Navy. He was assigned to a carrier in the Atlantic Fleet, and flew as a pilot for three years. After leaving the Navy in 1952, Shepard enrolled at the Naval Postgraduate School in Monterey, California, where he received a master's degree in aeronautical engineering. He was assigned to the Naval Air Test Center in Patuxent River, Maryland, where he conducted research on rocketry and missile guidance systems. In 1953, he married his wife, Janet Gaye, and they had a son, John, and a daughter, Katherine. Shepard was promoted to lieutenant in 1955. In 1957, he attended the National War College in Washington, D.C. Shepard was promoted to lieutenant commander in 1958. In 1961, he was promoted to commander. In 1962, he was assigned to the Naval Space Program in Washington, D.C. Shepard was promoted to captain in 1963. In 1964, he was assigned to the Naval Air Test Center in Patuxent River, Maryland. Shepard was promoted to commodore in 1968. In 1970, he was assigned to the U.S. Naval Research Laboratory in Washington, D.C., where he was promoted to rear admiral. In 1973, he was assigned to the U.S. Space and Naval Warfare Systems Center in San Diego, California. Shepard was promoted to vice admiral in 1974. In 1974, he retired

Liselotte Grschebina was a German-Jewish sculptor, who was born in Stuttgart on June 14, 1930, and died there in 1994. She was a student at the School of Applied Arts in Stuttgart from 1952 until 1956, and then worked in the studio of the sculptor Otto Demuth. In 1964, she was a guest at the Art Students League of New York, and in 1968, she won the first prize in the Art Academy of Düsseldorf. Grschebina's works are in the collections of the Israel Museum in Jerusalem, the Israel Museum of Art in Haifa, the Jewish Museum in Berlin, the Jewish Museum in New York, the Jewish Museum in Vienna, the Jewish Museum in Berlin, and the Jewish Museum in Budapest.

The ALCO RS-3 is a 17068.8 mm (69.0 in) long, four-door, four-passenger sedan with a rear-wheel drive layout. It was produced from 1953 to 1957 by American Locomotive Company. The RS-3 is a derivative of the ALCO RS-1, which was itself a variant of the ALCO RS-2. The RS-3 was used in the 1950s as a light executive car by a number of companies in the United States and Canada, including United States Lines, the American Bus Lines, the Canadian Pacific Railway and the Canadian National Railway. The car was also used as a shuttle bus by several companies including the Montreal Transit Company.  The RS-3 was powered by a 12-cylinder, side-valve, water-cooled 4.8-litre engine, which was later replaced by a 5.9-litre engine.  The car was produced in several versions, including a long-wheelbase sedan with a built-in rear bench seat, a two-door station wagon, and a short-wheelbase station wagon.  The car was produced with a number of different body styles, including sedan, station wagon and convertible.  The RS-3 was one of the first cars to use a hydraulic braking system, which replaced the older vacuum braking systems.  The RS-3 was one of the last cars produced by American Locomotive Company before it was merged with Baldwin Locomotive Works to form the Baldwin-ALCO Corporation.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Army and U.S. Air Force as a troop carrier and ambulance during the Korean War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Navy as a troop carrier and ambulance during the Korean War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Army as a command car and ambulance during the Vietnam War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Air Force as a command car during the Vietnam War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Army as an ambulance during the Vietnam War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Army as a command car during the Vietnam War.  The RS-3 was used by the U.S. Army as a command car during the Vietnam War. The Fellowship of the Ring by J. R. R. Tolkien The Fellowship of the Ring is a fantasy novel written by J. R. R. Tolkien. It is the first book in The Lord of the Rings trilogy. The Fellowship of the Ring was first published in the UK by George Allen & Unwin on 21 September 1954, and in the United States by Harper & Row on 3 September 1955. The Fellowship of the Ring was followed by The Two Towers and The Return of the King. The book was adapted into a motion picture by New Line Cinema, and released in 2001. The Fellowship of the Ring is set in the fictional world of Middle-earth. It is the story of a quest to destroy a powerful ring created by the Dark Lord Sauron. The Fellowship of the Ring was preceded by The Hobbit, a children's novel set in the same world, published in 1937. The Fellowship of the Ring was followed by The Two Towers and The Return of the King, which comprise The Lord of the Rings. The Lord of the Rings was published between 1954 and 1955, and has been translated into over forty languages. The Fellowship of the Ring is a fantasy novel, a genre that explores imaginary worlds and fantastic beings, and includes such sub-genres as science fiction, horror, and fairy tales. The Fellowship of the Ring was written by J. R. R. Tolkien, the pseudonym of the English author John Ronald Reuel Tolkien. Tolkien was born in Bloemfontein, South Africa, in 1892. He was educated at Exeter College, Oxford, and served in World War I. After the war, he became a professor at the University of Leeds. Tolkien was a prolific writer, and is best known for The Hobbit and The Lord of the Rings. He also wrote poetry, fiction, and essays. Tolkien was a member of the Inklings, a group of writers who met regularly to discuss their work. Tolkien died in 1973. Darinka Dentcheva is an optimization expert with a background in mathematics. She has been working at the Mathematics Department of the Humboldt University in Berlin since 1984. Her research interests are in the area of mathematical optimization and variational inequalities, with a focus on optimization methods for discrete and continuous problems. Her work has been published in several scientific journals and she has also been involved in several international projects. She has published more than 90 articles and delivered several keynote lectures and invited speeches at conferences. Darinka Dentcheva has a B.A. in Mathematics from the University of Sofia in Bulgaria and a Ph.D. from the Humboldt University in Berlin. She was also a visiting professor at the University of Pennsylvania in the United States. She was elected to the International Academy of Mathematical Optimization in 1997 and she is also an Honorary Member of the Bulgarian Academy of Sciences. She has won several awards including the Humboldt Prize in 1996 and the European Research Award in 2000. In addition, she has been a fellow of the Alexander von Humboldt Foundation, the Leibniz Association and the German Research Foundation. She has also been a member of the German Academy of Sciences Leopoldina and the German Academy of Sciences.

The Velvet Underground was an American rock band formed in 1964 in New York City. It was composed of singer-songwriter Lou Reed, guitarist Sterling Morrison, drummer Maureen Tucker, and bassist John Cale, who was later replaced by Doug Yule. The band's name came from Andy Warhol's 1967 film The Velvet Underground, which featured the band performing in a Warhol film. In the early 1970s, the Velvet Underground disbanded, with Reed forming the band Lou Reed and the Velvet Underground. Cale went on to found the band The Band. Reed and Morrison went on to form the band Reed's side project The Primitives.

Alan B. Miller Hall was designed by Robert A. M. Stern, an internationally renowned architect. The building opened on March 30, 2007, with a ribbon-cutting ceremony attended by College of William and Mary President David T. Hollister, Alan B. Miller, and other dignitaries. The building is named after Alan B. Miller, the founder of the College of William and Mary and the first president of the college. Mason School of Business moved into the building on October 1, 2007, and now occupies the entire building. The building is also home to the College of William and Mary's office of Institutional Advancement.

Olga Bondareva was born in Leningrad, USSR on 27 April 1937. She died in Saint Petersburg, Russia on 3 October 2014. She is known for her contribution to the field of mathematics. She was a mathematician who worked on several fields in mathematics. She was the first female to become a full professor of mathematics at a Russian university. She worked on the theory of Lie algebras and on Lie groups, and on the theory of algebraic groups. She was one of the founders of the Moscow Mathematical School, where she taught from 1970 until her retirement in 2000. She was a fellow of the American Mathematical Society. She was the author of several books and several hundred papers.

Mermaid Train Song The Mermaid Train Song is a Pop-rock song by Train. The song was released as the first single from Train's album Drops of Jupiter. It was written by band members Pat Monahan and Jim Wilson and produced by Train and John Shanks. The song peaked at number two on the US Billboard Hot 100 and number one on the Billboard Hot 100 Airplay chart. The song was certified triple platinum by the RIAA. It was also a number-one hit on the Billboard Adult Top 40 chart and the Billboard Hot Adult Contemporary Tracks chart. The song was a commercial success, reaching number one on the Hot 100 Airplay chart and number two on the Hot 100, becoming Train's second number-one single on the latter chart. The song was nominated for Best Pop Performance by a Duo or Group with Vocals at the 2009 Grammy Awards. The song is based on a true story of a man named Danny who was caught in a train wreck. Danny's girlfriend, who was with him at the time, survived the wreck, but he was not so fortunate. Danny's girlfriend later wrote a song about her experience, which she called the Mermaid Train Song. The song was also inspired by the story of a man named George who was caught in a train wreck. George survived the wreck, but his girlfriend, who was with him at the time, was not so fortunate. Noise rock is a music genre that emerged in the 1970s as a mutation of the rock music scene. It is characterized by experimental production techniques, noisy sound, and lyrical themes related to depression, isolation, and alienation. Post-hardcore is a subgenre of hardcore punk that emerged in the 1980s. It is characterized by a slower, more melodic sound, and lyrical themes related to social commentary and emotion.

Terence Rattigan was born in October 1905 in London, England, the second son of a successful actor-manager, Sir Henry Rattigan. His father's company toured the world for fifteen years. Rattigan was educated at Dulwich College and Trinity College, Cambridge. His first play, The Deep Blue Sea, premiered in 1933. It was followed by several successful plays, including A Man of No Importance, Love for Love, The Winslow Boy, and The Skin Game. He wrote a number of successful operas, including the light operas Iolanthe and The Merry Wives of Windsor, as well as the more serious operas The Little Foxes and The Tiger Lily. It is the second-oldest surviving university in the English-speaking world, after the University of Oxford, and the fourth-oldest in the world, after the Universities of Paris, Bologna, and Oxford. The University of Cambridge is a collegiate public research university, with 31 constituent colleges and over 30,000 students. Cambridge is the largest city in the East of England, with a population of over 350,000. The city is located on the River Cam, approximately 40 miles (64 km) north of London. The city is one of the most famous in the United Kingdom, with its name often used as a metonym for the university. The city is also home to the headquarters of the Natural History Museum, the Museum of Archaeology and Anthropology, and the Fitzwilliam Museum. It is also the home of the Cambridge University Library and the Cambridge University Press. Cambridge has been a university city since the 12th century. The city was the centre of the wool industry in the Middle Ages and the centre of the horse trading in the 19th century. The city has a large number of colleges, all of which have buildings of architectural significance. The University of Cambridge has a very large number of notable alumni, including 48 Nobel laureates, 7 Fields Medalists, 15 Turing Award winners, 3 Olympic gold medalists, 4 British prime ministers, and a large number of other notable scientists, politicians, writers, and musicians.  Cambridge is the birthplace of Isaac Newton, the father of modern physics. The city is also the birthplace of Charles Darwin, the father of modern evolutionary biology. The city is also the birthplace of Stephen Hawking, a physicist, cosmologist, and author who was a major figure in theoretical physics and a prominent advocate for the scientific study of the universe. The city is also the birthplace of Charles Babbage, a pioneer in the development of the mechanical computer. Cambridge is also the birthplace of Alan Turing, a mathematician who was a pioneer of computer science. The city is also the birthplace of J. R. R. Tolkien, the author of the famous fantasy books The Hobbit and The Lord of the Rings. The Honeymoon Killers are an American band from Portland, Oregon. They formed in 1984 and have released five albums. Their sound is characterized by heavy distorted guitars, loud vocals, and a distinct rhythm. They are often described as a punk rock band, but their music is often also compared to heavy metal and noise rock. The band's name is a reference to the movie The Honeymoon Killers, in which the main characters are serial killers.
Olga Nikolaevna Bondareva (born 30 July 1934), a Russian mathematician, is known for her contributions to the theory of graph coloring and to the theory of finite groups.  Bondareva is the author of a number of fundamental results on the chromatic number of graphs. In 1968 she proved that every bridgeless cubic graph has chromatic number at most four. In 1974 she proved that the chromatic number of any bridgeless cubic graph is either four or five. In 1976 she proved that every bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable unless it is isomorphic to a double cover of the Petersen graph. In the same paper she conjectured that every bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable. This conjecture was proved by Kenneth Appel and Wolfgang Haken in 1977. In a further paper in 1977, she proved that the graph of any bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable unless it is isomorphic to one of seven exceptional graphs. In 1978 she proved that every bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable unless it is isomorphic to one of three exceptional graphs. In 1982, she proved that every bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable unless it is isomorphic to one of five exceptional graphs. In 1997, she proved that every bridgeless cubic graph is 4-colorable unless it is isomorphic to one of five exceptional graphs.  Bondareva also made important contributions to the theory of finite groups. In 1973, together with A. Shapiro and V. Shapiro, she proved that if a finite group has a normal subgroup of index at most two, then it has a normal subgroup of index at most four. In 1979, she proved that if a finite group has a normal subgroup of index at most two, then it has a normal subgroup of index at most three. In 1980, together with A. Shapiro, she proved that if a finite group has a normal subgroup of index at most two, then it has a normal subgroup of index at most five. In 1984, together with A. Shapiro, she proved that if a finite group has a normal subgroup of index at most two, then it has a normal subgroup of index at most six.  Bondareva was born in Leningrad (now St. Petersburg), Soviet Union. She studied mathematics at the Leningrad State University, receiving a Ph.D. in 1964. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, located in the city of Madrid, and is the principal hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia. It is the eighth-busiest airport in Europe in terms of passenger traffic and the second-busiest in the European Union after London Heathrow Airport. It is the fourth-busiest airport in Europe in terms of cargo traffic and the second-busiest in the European Union after Frankfurt Airport. The airport is named after Adolfo Suárez, the first President of the Government of Spain after the death of Francisco Franco. It is the largest airport in Spain by area and one of the largest in the world by area of land occupied. It is the first airport in the world to have a single terminal with two runways. The airport was opened on 4 April 1931, with the first flight being a scheduled flight to Barcelona by the company Iberia. The airport was the main base for the Spanish Air Force until the 1970s and until the 1980s for the Spanish Air Force. It is the main hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia and for the Spanish low-cost carrier Vueling. The airport is served by almost 100 airlines. The deal, which includes publishing and sync licensing, is the label's first with an independent label. INgrooves, which was founded in 1995 by the late Irving Azoff, is one of the largest independent music distributors in the world. The deal will give INgrooves access to Warner Music Group's roster of artists, which includes Train, Incubus, Imagine Dragons, the Script, and The Lumineers. Olga Nikolaevna Bondareva (born 30 August 1934), a Russian mathematician, is known for her contributions to the theory of numbers, especially her proof of the Bondarev–Shapley theorem in the early 1960s. She was born in Saint Petersburg, the daughter of a teacher. She studied at the Leningrad State University, where she graduated in 1959. She was awarded a PhD degree in 1964, and a DSc degree in 1975. She was a professor of mathematics at the Leningrad State University from 1963 to 1982, and at the Lomonosov Moscow State University from 1982 until her retirement in 2000. She died in Saint Petersburg in 2018.

Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, serving the capital city of Madrid. It is located in the municipality of Alcobendas in the autonomous community of Madrid, Spain. It is the second busiest airport in Spain, serving over 35 million passengers a year and handling more than 1,000,000 tonnes of cargo. The airport is owned by the Spanish state-owned company Aena, which also owns and operates most of the Spanish airports. It is located 16 km (9.9 mi) northwest of Madrid city centre, at the northern edge of the city, and is the main airport serving Madrid. It is named after Adolfo Suárez, the first president of the Spanish Government after the death of Francisco Franco. It was the largest airport in the world by total area at the time of its opening in 1931. Its original name was Madrid-Cuatro Vientos Airport, after the nearby airfield, but it was renamed in 1939 to commemorate the first Spanish president after the Spanish Civil War, Adolfo Suárez. In 1931, the Spanish Republican Air Force moved its headquarters to the airport from Cuatro Vientos Air Base, and it was declared the main Spanish airport in 1933. The airport was also the main Spanish base for the Air Force during World War II. The airport was used for military purposes until the end of the Spanish Civil War, when it was nationalised by the Spanish Republican government. After the war, it was used by the Spanish Air Force, the Spanish Navy, and the Spanish Civil Aviation. During the Spanish Civil War, the airport was also the base of the Spanish Republican Air Force. It was the main base of the Spanish Air Force during World War II. In 1945, the Spanish Air Force moved its headquarters to Torrejón Airport. After the war, the Spanish Air Force moved to Torrejón Air Base, and the airport was used for civil aviation. It was also the base of the Spanish Navy until 1955. In 1954, the Spanish Civil Aviation moved its headquarters to the airport. In 1955, the Spanish Navy moved its headquarters to the airport, and the Spanish Civil Aviation moved to Cuatro Vientos Air Base. The airport was officially opened in March 1931, but the first flight was on 2 May 1931. It was originally named Madrid-Cuatro Vientos Airport, after the nearby airfield, but it was renamed in 1939 to commemorate the first Spanish president after the Spanish Civil War, Adolfo Suárez. Olga Nikolaevna Bondareva (born April 27, 1937 in Saint Petersburg, Russia) is a Russian mathematician. Bondareva was born in Saint Petersburg. She graduated from the University of Leningrad (now Saint Petersburg State University) in 1961, and received a PhD degree in 1965. In 1969 she became an associate professor, and in 1975 a professor. In 1993 she became a professor of the Steklov Institute of Mathematics. Bondareva is known for her work in complex analysis and geometric function theory, and for her book Theory of Functions of a Complex Variable (with N. I. Muskhelishvili, M. I. Shabat, A. P. Korolev). She is a member of the Russian Academy of Sciences, and is a corresponding member of the Academy of Sciences of the Czech Republic. She was elected to the European Academy of Sciences in 1997. She is the President of the Russian Mathematical Society. Bondareva died in Saint Petersburg on October 31, 2006.

University of Burgundy campus Dijon  The University of Burgundy is a public university located in Dijon, France. It was founded in 1423 as a Faculty of Arts and Medicine of the University of Paris. In 1722, it was granted the status of university. The university has three campuses, two in Dijon and one in Chenôve. It has about 18,000 students and more than 2,000 employees. Its mission is to teach, to research and to transfer knowledge. The university has a long tradition of research in the field of natural sciences. It is the largest university in the Burgundy region. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, located in the city of Madrid, and is the principal hub for Spanish flag carrier Iberia. It is owned by the Spanish air navigation service provider ENAIRE, a public company owned by the Ministry of Development. The airport is located in the municipality of Alcobendas, a district of the city of Madrid, in the Community of Madrid, Spain. The airport is named after the Spanish politician Adolfo Suárez. The airport is located at a distance of 6 km (3.7 mi) from the city of Madrid and has a total area of 1,860 hectares (4,730 acres). The airport is located in a valley, surrounded by mountains. The airport has two runways, which are parallel and oriented north-south. The longest runway is runway 04L-22R, which is 3,500 metres (11,483 ft) long and is used by all types of aircraft. The second runway, runway 04R-22L, is 3,400 metres (11,155 ft) long. The airport is equipped with a CAT III B instrument landing system. The airport has two terminals, Terminal 1 and Terminal 4, with a total of 110 gates. It handles a total of 45.5 million passengers per year. In 2011, the airport handled a total of 44,722,093 passengers, a 10.5% increase over the previous year. It handled a total of 1,774,849 aircraft movements in 2011, a 10.5% increase over the previous year. The airport is also the second busiest airport in Europe in terms of cargo, with a total of 1.8 million tons of cargo handled in 2011. The airport has a total of 70,000 parking spaces. The airport has a single railway station, located at Terminal 4. It is served by Cercanías Madrid, a commuter rail service operated by Renfe, and by Metro de Madrid, a rapid transit system operated by Metro de Madrid. It also has bus connections to the city of Madrid. The airport has two heliports, located at Terminal 1 and Terminal 4. The airport is served by a total of 127 airlines. The airport has a total of 2,400 employees. The airport is the base for Iberia Express, a low-cost subsidiary of Iberia, and for Air Nostrum, a regional airline. The Velvet Underground was an American rock band formed in 1964 in New York City. It was composed of singer/songwriter Lou Reed, guitarist Sterling Morrison, drummer Maureen Tucker, and bassist John Cale, who was later replaced by Doug Yule. The band's name was taken from Andy Warhol's 1964 film, The Velvet Underground. Their music was heavily influenced by the blues, the avant-garde, and the art rock of the time. The band's debut album, The Velvet Underground & Nico, was released in March 1967. Although the album was critically acclaimed, it was not a commercial success. However, it was widely influential, and has been called the "first punk rock album." The Velvet Underground was one of the first bands to produce its own album artwork, including the iconic banana-shaped cover of their debut album. The band's recordings were notable for their experimental production and sound, which included the use of feedback and noise, and the absence of a traditional rhythm section. Reed's songwriting and lyrics were also notable for their unconventional style, which has been called "brooding, dark, and introspective." Reed's distinctive voice, characterized by a high-pitched timbre and a tendency to pronounce words as syllables, became a defining characteristic of the band. The band had several line-up changes throughout its existence. Reed and Morrison left the band in 1968, and were replaced by guitarist Angus MacLise and bassist Maureen Tucker. Tucker left the band in 1969, and was replaced by Doug Yule. The band recorded two more studio albums in 1969 and 1970, before disbanding. The Velvet Underground reunited in 1973 and recorded two more albums. The band broke up for a second time in 1974. In 1998, the band was inducted into the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame. The Velvet Underground was one of the most influential bands of the 1960s. Its music was highly influential on the development of punk rock, new wave, alternative rock, and heavy metal. It was also influential on the development of hip hop. The Velvet Underground's songs have been covered by numerous artists, including U2, Nirvana, the Pixies, and the Beastie Boys. Reed's songwriting style has been particularly influential, and his work has been cited as an influence by several artists, including David

Brandon Carter is a professional astrophysicist specializing in general relativity. He is currently a professor of physics at the University of Maryland, College Park. He is a former research scientist at the National Science Foundation and a former research scientist at the Goddard Space Flight Center of the National Aeronautics and Space Administration. He received his B.S. in physics from the University of California, Santa Barbara in 1987, and his Ph.D. in physics from the University of California, Los Angeles in 1993. He is a member of the American Physical Society, the European Physical Society, and the American Astronomical Society. He has served as the chair of the Department of Physics at the University of Maryland since September 2014. His primary research interests are in the areas of general relativity, cosmology, and mathematical physics. In addition to his research, he is a widely published popular science author. He is the author of the books The End of Time and Time Reborn, which together have sold over 500,000 copies worldwide. He has also written numerous articles for magazines and newspapers such as the New York Times, the Washington Post, and the Los Angeles Times. He was named to the National Academy of Sciences in 2015. He is the son of a physicist and a psychologist. He is married to a chemist. He lives in College Park, Maryland. He is a descendant of Thomas Carter and William Henry Carter. He is a distant relative of the 19th-century poet Robert Browning.

The ALCO RS-3 was built by Montreal Locomotive Works (MLW) in Montreal, Canada from 1950 to 1956. It is a 4-4-0 locomotive. The RS-3 was built to replace the RS-2 and RS-1. The RS-3 has a maximum speed of 88 km/h and a maximum tractive effort of 75,000 lbf. The RS-3 has a maximum weight of about 165 tonnes.  The RS-3 is a very popular locomotive and is found in many railroads, including the Canadian National Railway, Canadian Pacific Railway, Quebec North Shore & Labrador Railway, and the New York Central Railroad. The RS-3 has been retired from most of its original operations, but it is still used on the New York Central Railroad.  The RS-3 was also built in Australia by Australian Locomotive Company. She is an author and co-author of over 100 research papers in these fields, and has worked in the areas of the mathematics of finance and stochastic control. She has held academic positions in the United States, Canada, Italy and Bulgaria, and was a visiting professor at several universities. She is currently a full professor at the University of California, Berkeley. She is also a member of the European Association of Operational Research Societies, the Operational Research Society of the Czech Republic, and the Bulgarian Academy of Sciences. She has been a member of the editorial board of several international journals. In 2010, she was elected a member of the European Academy of Sciences and Arts. She was elected a Fellow of the Institute of Mathematics and Applications of the Romanian Academy in 2012. The Quine Tapes recordedIn United States  The Quine Tapes is a bootleg series of recordings of various live performances by the American singer-songwriter Bob Dylan. The bootlegs were originally released in the United States on a variety of labels, most of which have since been out of print. The first release was in January 1967, by Bob Dylan's former manager Albert Grossman and his new company, Albert Grossman Entertainment, and was released under the title The Quine Tapes, Volume 1. The release was a double-LP set containing live recordings from a 1967 concert at the Capitol Theatre in Port Chester, New York, on December 26, 1966. The album was later reissued on CD in the 1990s by Albert Grossman Entertainment, with the title The Quine Tapes, Volume 1. The original album was reissued in the UK in 1999 by Music On Vinyl. The album was also released as The Quine Tapes, Volume 1 on compact disc by Big Time Records in 2005, and again in 2007 by Alligator Records, with a bonus track. The album was also released in 2009 by Music On Vinyl, again with a bonus track. Brandon Carter, PhD, is the Thomas B. Ford Professor of Physics at the University of Chicago. He is a theoretical physicist who has made seminal contributions to cosmology, quantum gravity, and string theory. He is the author of numerous articles and several books, including The Doomsday Argument (Oxford University Press, 1998), a popularization of cosmology that has been translated into several languages.  He was born in England and earned his PhD from the University of Cambridge in 1969. In 1970, he joined the faculty at Princeton University as a member of the Institute for Advanced Study. He became a full professor at the University of Chicago in 1973 and is now the Ford Professor of Physics.  Carter is a Fellow of the American Physical Society and the American Association for the Advancement of Science. He has served as president of the American Physical Society and the American Association for the Advancement of Science, and as chairman of the Board of Directors of the American Museum of Natural History.  Carter is married to the economist Esther Duflo, and they have three children. He lives in Hyde Park, Chicago.

Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is the main airport of Spain, serving the capital city of Madrid. It is located in the municipality of Alcobendas in the autonomous community of Madrid, Spain. It is the second busiest airport in Spain, and the sixth busiest in Europe. Adolfo Suárez Madrid-Barajas Airport is also the fourth busiest airport in the world by cargo traffic, and the sixth busiest in the world by passenger traffic. It is owned and operated by ENAIRE, the Spanish state-owned air navigation service provider, which is a part of the Ministry of Transport, Mobility and Urban Agenda. The airport is located at an elevation of 610 metres (2,000 ft) above sea level. The airport has two runways, 14L/32R and 4L/22R. The longest runway is the 14L/32R, which is 4,200 metres (13,800 ft) long.  The airport is named after Adolfo Suárez, 1st President of the Government of Spain from 1976 to 1981, and former President of the Spanish Council of State from 1981 to his death in 2014. The airport is also known as Madrid-Barajas Airport, Madrid-Barajas, Madrid-Barajas International Airport, and simply Barajas Airport. The airport is also known as Adolfo Suárez Madrid–Barajas Airport.  It is the main international airport of Spain, serving Madrid, the capital city of Spain.  The airport has a large terminal complex, which is divided into terminals T1, T2 and T3.  The airport is the main hub of Iberia, Spain's national carrier.
